<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer927986065352263822">The fascinating, true story of the world's deadliest disease. <br />In 1918, the Great Flu Epidemic felled the young and healthy virtually overnight. An estimated forty million people died as the epidemic raged. Children were left orphaned and families were devastated. As many American soldiers were killed by the 1918 flu as were killed in battle during World War I. And no area</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText927986065352263822">The fascinating, true story of the world's deadliest disease. <br />In 1918, the Great Flu Epidemic felled the young and healthy virtually overnight. An estimated forty million people died as the epidemic raged. Children were left orphaned and families were devastated. As many American soldiers were killed by the 1918 flu as were killed in battle during World War I. And no area of the globe was safe. Eskimos living in remote outposts in the frozen tundra were sickened and killed by the flu in such numbers that entire villages were wiped out. <br />Scientists have recently rediscovered shards of the flu virus frozen in Alaska and preserved in scraps of tissue in a government warehouse. Gina Kolata, an acclaimed reporter for "The New York Times," unravels the mystery of this lethal virus with the high drama of a great adventure story. Delving into the history of the flu and previous epidemics, detailing the science and the latest understanding of this mortal disease, Kolata addresses the prospects for a great epidemic recurring, and, most important, what can be done to prevent it.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="927986065352263822">...more</a>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_524829578">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/524829578" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten" class="left imgcol" title="Jeffrey Keeten"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1301087352p2/3427339.jpg" alt="Jeffrey Keeten" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/524829578?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 25, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten" class="user" title="Jeffrey Keeten">Jeffrey Keeten</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer524829578">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer184047442248536027"><strong>”This is a detective story. Here was a mass murderer that was around 80 years ago and who’s never been brought to justice. And what we’re trying to do is find the murderer.”--Jeffery Taubenberger, molecular pathologist</strong><br /><br />There are estimates that the 1918 Flu killed anywhere from 20 million to 100 million people dwarfing the number of people killed in World War One. Either number is horrifying, but as modern scientists start putting data together the larger number becomes more realistic. I’ve always</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText184047442248536027"><strong>”This is a detective story. Here was a mass murderer that was around 80 years ago and who’s never been brought to justice. And what we’re trying to do is find the murderer.”--Jeffery Taubenberger, molecular pathologist</strong><br /><br />There are estimates that the 1918 Flu killed anywhere from 20 million to 100 million people dwarfing the number of people killed in World War One. Either number is horrifying, but as modern scientists start putting data together the larger number becomes more realistic. I’ve always been fascinated with the 1918 Flu outbreak for a number of reasons, but the one that really sticks with me is that we never defeated it. We never knocked it to the canvas. It came, it killed, it disappeared.<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/1918Flu_zps4d4c6060.jpg.html">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt=" photo 1918Flu_zps4d4c6060.jpg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380407902i/797295.jpg" /></a><br /><br /><em>”Historian Alfred W. Crosby remarks that whatever the exact number felled by the 1918 flu, one thing is indisputable: the virus killed more humans than any other disease in a period of similar duration in the history of the world.”</em><br /><br />That is a big statement. It makes the Black Plague look like a featherweight. <em>”How lethal was it? It was twenty-five times more deadly than ordinary influenzas. This flu killed 2.5 percent of its victims. Normally just one-tenth of 1 percent of people who get the flu die. And since a fifth of the world’s population got the flu that year, including 28 percent of Americans, the number of deaths was stunning. So many died, in fact, that the average life span in the United States fell by twelve years in 1918. If such a plague came today, killing a similar fraction of the U. S. population, 1.5 million Americans would die.”</em><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Influenza_zpsebb33bdd.jpg.html">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt=" photo Influenza_zpsebb33bdd.jpg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380407902i/797296.jpg" /></a><br /><strong>1918 Influenza Virus</strong><br /><br />Interest was reignited in the 1918 influenza outbreak when swine flu/bird flu showed up in the 1970s and again in the 1990s. China is a hot bed for new influenza bugs because of the proximity of birds/swine/humans. Many times you find all three species under the same roof. Birds cannot pass flu to humans, but they can pass it to swine. Swine, being a close genetic relative to humans, (not that surprising) can incubate a bird flu and pass it to humans. The moral of the story is that pigs, birds and humans should not wallow in the same mud hole. The current thought is that the 1918 flu came to humans via pigs via birds. <br /><br /><em>”In theory, a bird flu could not infect a human because the virus should require cellular enzymes found in bird intestinal cells but not in human lung cells. Yet if, against all odds, a bird flu virus was infecting people, it would have hemagglutinin and neuraminidase proteins that had never been seen before by a human being. No human would be immune to such a virus. The whole world was at risk.”</em><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/CutePig_zps01e59529.jpg.html">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt=" photo CutePig_zps01e59529.jpg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380407902i/797297.jpg" /></a><br /><strong>I know he is cute, but he is a deadly assassin.</strong><br /><br />So there is this very unfortunate pig who becomes infected with a human virus and a bird virus at the same time. He becomes a blender for these two viruses and the next time a human scratches him behind the ears, most likely a child (wonderful incubators), he will pass the new concoction on to humanity which is tragic on many levels, but for the pig especially because who will fill his slop trough if his humans are critically sick. <br /><br />Before HIV appeared on the scene which would shift all infectious disease researchers in that direction there were teams of scientists searching for samples of the 1918 flu. As is the case with a publish or perish society scientists are not very good at sharing informations, so as one team goes to Alaska to look for victims of the 1918 flu, hopefully still frozen in permafrost, another team is planning to go to an island of Norway with the same thought. When the Alaska team finds a perfectly preserved specimen that information of course is not shared with the rivals even though there was a scientist coordinating both teams. Johan Hultin is the man who makes the find.<br /><br /><em>”She was an obese woman; she had fat in her skin and around her organs and that served as a protection from the occasional short-term thawing of permafrost.” Hultin explained. “Those on either side of her were not obese and they had decayed. I sat on the pail and saw this woman in a state of good preservation. And I knew that this was where the virus has got to come from, shedding light on the mysteries of 1918.”</em><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" target="_blank" href="http://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/JohanHultin_zps9b2d045d.jpg.html">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt=" photo JohanHultin_zps9b2d045d.jpg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380407902i/797298.jpg" /></a><br /><strong>Johan Hultin virus detective.</strong><br /><br />I would hope, and firmly believe that if the world was on the brink of a major pandemic that scientists would pool their research and share any breakthroughs before publishing (being credited) their findings. During the course of this investigation they also found paraffin preserved lung tissue from victims of the 1918 flu stored at the National Tissue Repository maintained by the Armed Forces Institute of Pathology. Nice to know we have such a handy repository of our disease history. <br /><br />When a deadly influenza swine flu virus showed up in 1976 President Gerald Ford took the initiative ( I know right who would have thunk it.) that for the first time in human history the government was going to try and immunize the whole country. The press was favorable in the beginning of the program, but papers like the New York Post started to turn the tide towards government conspiracy theories. They wrote on October 14th and article <em>That spoke of a seventy-five-year-old woman who winced at the sting of the hypodermic, then had taken a few feeble steps and dropped dead.</em> Then on October 25th, <em>”the paper suggested that Carol Gambino, the mobster, had been killed by the Mafia using a swine flu shot as the deadly weapon”.</em> <br /><br />These misguided, uninformed, paranoid beliefs are laughable, but with politicians like Michelle Bachman and with radio talk show hosts like Rush Limbaugh who are suspicious of any government programs, especially if a Democrat is in the White House, and are very loud about their opposition; I’m sure a similar program to try and stop a nasty flu bug before it got started would be met with heavy unwarranted criticism that could ultimately cost a lot of lives. If the 1918 influenza were to appear today we have antibiotics to counter the bacteria that floods the weakened lungs (pneumonia killed as many or more people than the virus) of a virus ridden body so death counts would be reduced from the 1918 level, but due to the efforts of a handful of scientists we do have the ability now to immunize a population if they will let us. <br /><br />Gina Kolata has taken me on an investigative adventure that not only made science fascinating, but also accessible. I’m scared, but less scared because I have confidence in the ability of our best and brightest to keep the worst nightmares at bay if only we give them the means and we listen to them before the tip over point has been attained. <br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="184047442248536027">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_29225790">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/29225790" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1354820-ned-ryerson" class="left imgcol" title="Ned Ryerson"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1217395068p2/1354820.jpg" alt="Ned Ryerson" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/29225790?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 04, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1354820-ned-ryerson" class="user" title="Ned Ryerson">Ned Ryerson</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/763331.Flu" title="Paperback 9780743203982" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer29225790">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5234598054553530784">I love a good disease book. And I think the 1918 flu is just about as fascinating as you can get. But this book talks more about theories and old-timey labs than it does about the human side of this epidemic. Which, let's face it, is what's really interesting. Imagine all of a sudden having a common illness sweep through your community and kill young healthy people so fast that you don't even have time to bury them right. That's some serious shit. This book just didn't do it justice. I would lik</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5234598054553530784">I love a good disease book. And I think the 1918 flu is just about as fascinating as you can get. But this book talks more about theories and old-timey labs than it does about the human side of this epidemic. Which, let's face it, is what's really interesting. Imagine all of a sudden having a common illness sweep through your community and kill young healthy people so fast that you don't even have time to bury them right. That's some serious shit. This book just didn't do it justice. I would like to find another that maybe does a better job.<br /><br />That being said, this lady's name is Gina Kolata. If you like Gina Kolata, and getting caught in the rain... </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_570497150">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/570497150" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/15736557-lynne-king" class="left imgcol" title="Lynne King"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1357126217p2/15736557.jpg" alt="Lynne King" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

<span id="freeTextContainer13019440327074940296">Right now, I'm thoroughly enjoying this read by NYT reporter Gina Kolata - it does seem odd that with the impact of the 1918 flu we haven't heard more about it or how it changed American life as we know it.<br /><br />I had no idea Katherine Anne Porter's Pale Horse, Pale Rider dealt with this topic, nor Thomas Wolfe's Look Homeward, Angel, so I am going to now read these two books after this one with a different context and knowledge base - which I hope will give me a deeper appreciation for both. <br /><br />I'll be</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13019440327074940296">Right now, I'm thoroughly enjoying this read by NYT reporter Gina Kolata - it does seem odd that with the impact of the 1918 flu we haven't heard more about it or how it changed American life as we know it.<br /><br />I had no idea Katherine Anne Porter's Pale Horse, Pale Rider dealt with this topic, nor Thomas Wolfe's Look Homeward, Angel, so I am going to now read these two books after this one with a different context and knowledge base - which I hope will give me a deeper appreciation for both. <br /><br />I'll be back with my concluding thoughts.<br /><br />Okay, I'm back. I think this was a good, fast-paced interesting read until the end, when it devolved into scientific political struggles and a muddled message. I found it difficult to keep interest, but finished it because I felt I had invested so much in the story to that point, however the ending left a lot to be desired. It did, however, have a lot of end notes and an extensive bibliography.</span>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer41043360">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1492672805792564688">An interesting look at a part of our history that can get glossed over sometimes. Unfortunately, this was focused more on the science that went into deciphering the flu rather than the history of the flu itself. While it was an enlightening read, and some of the people who worked on this project were extremely driven, fascinating people, mostly it just made me want to read a good old fashioned history book about the influenza pandemic.<br /><br />My one real issue was the completely unnecessary pages of lis</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1492672805792564688">An interesting look at a part of our history that can get glossed over sometimes. Unfortunately, this was focused more on the science that went into deciphering the flu rather than the history of the flu itself. While it was an enlightening read, and some of the people who worked on this project were extremely driven, fascinating people, mostly it just made me want to read a good old fashioned history book about the influenza pandemic.<br /><br />My one real issue was the completely unnecessary pages of lists that Kolata put in - naming every. single. doctor. who attended a particular conference, for instance. It's a waste of space and it mostly just made me drift, especially when two thirds of those doctors and scientists were never mentioned again.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_731590470">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/731590470" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7757273-lynn-g" class="left imgcol" title="Lynn G."><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1441585597p2/7757273.jpg" alt="Lynn G." /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/731590470?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 08, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7757273-lynn-g" class="user" title="Lynn G.">Lynn G.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7757273-lynn-g?shelf=medicine-psychology" class="actionLinkLite">medicine-psychology</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7757273-lynn-g?shelf=non-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7757273-lynn-g?shelf=books-read-in-2013" class="actionLinkLite">books-read-in-2013</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7757273-lynn-g?shelf=science" class="actionLinkLite">science</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer731590470">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7244499404452030521">I thought that this informative book about an interesting topic, the influenza epidemic of 1918, made some complex scientific processes approachable by the lay reader. The book reads almost like a biography of influenza; informing readers about previous epidemics/pandemics; similarities and differences between known influenzas; the attempts, both failed and successful, to identify and isolate the various molecular fragments of the viral genes. The focus, of course, was what differentiated the 19</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7244499404452030521">I thought that this informative book about an interesting topic, the influenza epidemic of 1918, made some complex scientific processes approachable by the lay reader. The book reads almost like a biography of influenza; informing readers about previous epidemics/pandemics; similarities and differences between known influenzas; the attempts, both failed and successful, to identify and isolate the various molecular fragments of the viral genes. The focus, of course, was what differentiated the 1918 pandemic from those previous. Why was it so deadly? How did it spread? How was it able to spread so quickly around the world? Who was vulnerable? Who wasn't? Why? Why? Why?<br /><br />As of the date of publication, 1999, there were tantalizing bits of the mystery that were being slowly revealed through a myriad of scientific investigative techniques made possible by advances in technologies, but the major questions had still not been answered. I am curious enough to look further into the progress made in the intervening years. <br /><br />Some interesting facts I gleaned from the book are: most flu strains originate in or around Guangdong (formerly Canton), China; the 1918 pandemic killed more than 20 million people in India, alone; there is a connection between the 1918 influenza and swine flu; in an attempt to find extant virus from 1918 flu victims, two different groups of scientists exhumed the graves of people known to have died of the flu. These graves were in permafrost areas of Alaska and Spitsbergen (a region of Norway just 800 miles from the Arctic Circle). The thought was that bodies buried in the permafrost, even decades ago, would deteriorate very, very slowly allowing for the possibility that soft tissue from the lungs of those corpses would still exist, samples of which could be taken and tested, using new technologies that, hopefully, would reveal the virus that caused the flu. <br /><br />All in all FLU was a good read and very informative. If you are interested in science, medicine, or history this could be a book for you.<br /><br />One criticism that I have of the book is that the author repeated herself a number of times, using phrasing that she had used before when writing about the same topic. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7244499404452030521">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_63445209">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/63445209" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2027950-justin" class="left imgcol" title="Justin"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1428434221p2/2027950.jpg" alt="Justin" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/63445209?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 31, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2027950-justin" class="user" title="Justin">Justin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/763331.Flu" title="Paperback 9780743203982" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2027950-justin?shelf=historical" class="actionLinkLite">historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer63445209">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer802259992315850479">The book was published in 1999, but it reads like it was written just a few weeks ago. The information Gina presents is so relevant to today that it's eerie. I am fascinated by the parts of history that our textbooks seem to forget, and the 1918 flu is probably one of the largest omissions in our historical texts. In it's two phases ( lighter spring outbreak, followed by the massively deadly fall outbreak) it managed to decrease the world population significantly and took out more lives than WW1</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText802259992315850479">The book was published in 1999, but it reads like it was written just a few weeks ago. The information Gina presents is so relevant to today that it's eerie. I am fascinated by the parts of history that our textbooks seem to forget, and the 1918 flu is probably one of the largest omissions in our historical texts. In it's two phases ( lighter spring outbreak, followed by the massively deadly fall outbreak) it managed to decrease the world population significantly and took out more lives than WW1 and WW2 combined. Utterly devastating and completely forgotten in popular culture. The book starts quickly diving right into historical narrative of the time. the first 5 or so chapters are fascinating, but start to drag a bit when the topic switches to the 1970's swine flu debacle. The book picks up again when the author starts telling the story of modern day scientists investigating the near century old virus. Gina does a great job in putting the potentially boring scientific details into a story that is both informative and entertaining. I highly recommend this book for any history buff, or even anyone interested gaining in depth perspective on what's happening with the H1N1 virus outbreak happening right now.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="802259992315850479">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_33139361">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/33139361" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/345066-schnaucl" class="left imgcol" title="Schnaucl"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1190261548p2/345066.jpg" alt="Schnaucl" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/33139361?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 19, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/345066-schnaucl" class="user" title="Schnaucl">Schnaucl</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/763331.Flu" title="Paperback 9780743203982" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone interested in the Spanish Flu, virology, or pandemics</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/345066-schnaucl?shelf=library" class="actionLinkLite">library</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/345066-schnaucl?shelf=non-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/345066-schnaucl?shelf=read_2008" class="actionLinkLite">read_2008</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/345066-schnaucl?shelf=history" class="actionLinkLite">history</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/345066-schnaucl?shelf=september" class="actionLinkLite">september</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer33139361">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13345117823955768160">I really enjoyed this book. The book covers a range of time from the beginning of the 1918-19 flu right up to still lingering questions about what made that particular flu strain so deadly and why it affected the young and healthy as much as the elderly and very young.<br /><br />I really learned a lot about the Flu and about the fight to determine its origins and genetic composition. Some of the things in this book mirrored [Book:The Great Influenza:The Epic Story of the Deadliest Plague in History], altho</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13345117823955768160">I really enjoyed this book. The book covers a range of time from the beginning of the 1918-19 flu right up to still lingering questions about what made that particular flu strain so deadly and why it affected the young and healthy as much as the elderly and very young.<br /><br />I really learned a lot about the Flu and about the fight to determine its origins and genetic composition. Some of the things in this book mirrored [Book:The Great Influenza:The Epic Story of the Deadliest Plague in History], although I've found that of the three or four books I've read recently, none of them mention one symptom that <a title="John M. Barry" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=John%20M.%20Barry">John M. Barry</a> focused on which is a sometimes altered personality for Flu survivors. (In fact, Barry attributes Woodrow Wilson's treatment of the League of Nations to a personalty change brought on by the Flu). <br /><br />Flu is so rich I hardly know where to begin. In the course of reading the book I learned about a national repository of tissues samples which was put into place by Abraham Lincoln (the thing is beyond huge and a treasure trove of information), a massive and ultimately failed attempt to vaccinate the American public against a swine flu in the 1970s (which may well be a partial explanation for why some people so mistrust vaccines), and the route another deadly flu is likely to take. I gained an understanding of why some in the medical community were so worried about avian flu. It's thought that the 1918-19 flu may well have started in bird, passed to pigs where it mixed with a human flu, and was then transmitted to humans. I also have a better understanding of why (nearly?) every strain of flu has started in southeast Asia (it has to do with a farming system that encourages birds and pigs raised in close proximity). <br /><br />One of the most fascinating aspects of the book (for me) was that in the beginning when the first group of people attempted to dig up corpses long buried in permafrost with absolutely no protection whatsoever or even any thought about the fact that they might unleash another wave of the 1918-19 flu and kill millions, it seems horrible. By the end the amazing precautions another group wants to take when trying to do the same thing seem downright silly. <br /><br />I also think it's very interesting that the people who made the biggest strides in uncovering the origins and genetic structure of the Spanish Flu were not scientists who had made studying the Flu their focus. Indeed, one of the people who helped tremendously was a "lowly" lab technician.<br /><br />The end of the book is in some ways very frustrating because there is still no answer to why that particular flu was so deadly and there were a good five or six promising investigations that were started or yet to be started and I really wanted to know how they turned out. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13345117823955768160">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1195852803">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13900398943868352460">Starting with history and moving towards modern science, this is a great book for anyone interested in understanding influenza. Although the 1918 pandemic isn't discussed frequently, I really enjoyed learning about it not only because of its virulence but also because it explains what the designations H1N1 etc. actually mean, why we need so many flu vaccines every year, and why so many new strains of flu are discovered in China. Definitely fascinating with very light descriptions of the genetic</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13900398943868352460">Starting with history and moving towards modern science, this is a great book for anyone interested in understanding influenza. Although the 1918 pandemic isn't discussed frequently, I really enjoyed learning about it not only because of its virulence but also because it explains what the designations H1N1 etc. actually mean, why we need so many flu vaccines every year, and why so many new strains of flu are discovered in China. Definitely fascinating with very light descriptions of the genetic and molecular biology involved in retrieving long-dead diseases. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_136694266">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/136694266" itemprop="url" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/136694266?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 07, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3386809-christie" class="user" title="Christie">Christie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3386809-christie?shelf=history" class="actionLinkLite">history</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3386809-christie?shelf=mysteries" class="actionLinkLite">mysteries</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3386809-christie?shelf=20th-century" class="actionLinkLite">20th-century</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3386809-christie?shelf=books-with-pictures" class="actionLinkLite">books-with-pictures</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer136694266">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4469874922787854137"><strong> <em>When the plague came, on those chilly days of autumn, some said it was a terrible new weapon of war.</em> </strong><br /><br />In 1918, a pandemic hit the world and killed millions of people from China all the way out to the most remote outposts of the Alaskan wilderness. A world already reeling from the disastrous effects of the first World War had to deal with their young people dropping dead from a terrifying illness that cost more American lives than WWI, WWII, Korea, and Vietnam combined. But what caused this terri</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4469874922787854137"><strong>
  <em>When the plague came, on those chilly days of autumn, some said it was a terrible new weapon of war.</em>
</strong><br /><br />In 1918, a pandemic hit the world and killed millions of people from China all the way out to the most remote outposts of the Alaskan wilderness. A world already reeling from the disastrous effects of the first World War had to deal with their young people dropping dead from a terrifying illness that cost more American lives than WWI, WWII, Korea, and Vietnam combined. But what caused this terrible pandemic? Science is still searching for the virus that caused millions of deaths. In this book, Gina Kolata tells the story of the scientists searching for answers.<br /><br />I picked this book up because after reading <a title="In the Shadow of Blackbirds by Cat Winters" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13112915.In_the_Shadow_of_Blackbirds">In the Shadow of Blackbirds</a> and watching a Downton Abbey episode about the flu epidemic I was interested in the history of the pandemic and what life was like during the time period. Unfortunately only the first chapter is about 1918 and the rest is about the scientists who are trying to isolate the 1918 flu virus. Its not hugely disappointing, but I was wanting more about 1918 itself.<br /><br />The book is pretty fascinating. I learned a great deal about the process of making vaccines and discovering viruses. There are a great deal of anecdotes that do connect near the end and were very well done. I liked that the book compared the epidemic and the search for the virus to other disease and epidemics. <br /><br />I recommend this book to those who enjoy medical history and reading about diseases. It is a fascinating read if a bit dated (I would like to find an up-to-date book on the topic). </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4469874922787854137">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_65064373">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/65064373" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2485937-alan-marchant" class="left imgcol" title="Alan Marchant"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1247361337p2/2485937.jpg" alt="Alan Marchant" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/65064373?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 26, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2485937-alan-marchant" class="user" title="Alan Marchant">Alan Marchant</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/763331.Flu" title="Paperback 9780743203982" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2485937-alan-marchant?shelf=nonfiction" class="actionLinkLite">nonfiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer65064373">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9936066118651955091">Get sick, Get well, Hang around the inkwell.<br /><br />First the good. This timely and credible treatment of influenza fills a critical void. The book is very readable. Although concentrating on historical vignettes to the exclusion of scientific explanations, the book provides a helpful background for the consideration of risk, public policy, and personal preparation that arise from confusing, contradictory, and incomplete news items about flu outbreaks and related public health initiatives.<br /><br />Kolata clearly</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9936066118651955091">Get sick, Get well, Hang around the inkwell.<br /><br />First the good. This timely and credible treatment of influenza fills a critical void. The book is very readable. Although concentrating on historical vignettes to the exclusion of scientific explanations, the book provides a helpful background for the consideration of risk, public policy, and personal preparation that arise from confusing, contradictory, and incomplete news items about flu outbreaks and related public health initiatives.<br /><br />Kolata clearly communicates the uncertainties in current understanding of how the flu virus evolves and flue epidemics spread. But she is even-handed to a fault in her descriptions of competing theories and scientists - showing for example way too much patience for the narcissistic Kirsty Duncan.<br /><br />The major faults in this book are defects of omission. On the policy side, Kolata describes the epidemic of bogus lawsuits that arose from the swine flu scare, but she neglects to follow through with information about the indemnification laws and the excessive industry consolidation that followed. While inferring that dangerous new flu strains emerge from Southeast Asia, she makes no effort to address the distinct possibility of stopping the cycle by regulating China's poultry industry. On the scientific side, Kolata provided no information about the process of "reassortment" that drives the evolution of flu viruses. Also conspicuously absent is clear advice about how to minimize the risks of influenza.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9936066118651955091">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/115283943" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/942053-shannon" class="left imgcol" title="Shannon"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1378260736p2/942053.jpg" alt="Shannon" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/115283943?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 04, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/942053-shannon" class="user" title="Shannon">Shannon</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/763331.Flu" title="Paperback 9780743203982" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer115283943">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18208523637193159878">This was on the shelf at the library when I went to get The Great Influenza so I picked it up too. I read this one first---it was shorter. While the basis of the book was the 1918 Influenza, the real story was what happened in science and medicine afterward. While influenza was a known disease, the cause was not yet understood. There were no microscopes powerful enough to see a virus, and by the time anyone thought that might be the cause, the flu was gone, seemingly lost forever. The bulk of th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18208523637193159878">This was on the shelf at the library when I went to get The Great Influenza so I picked it up too. I read this one first---it was shorter. While the basis of the book was the 1918 Influenza, the real story was what happened in science and medicine afterward. While influenza was a known disease, the cause was not yet understood. There were no microscopes powerful enough to see a virus, and by the time anyone thought that might be the cause, the flu was gone, seemingly lost forever. The bulk of this book was the scientists who hunted for it and finally found it. <br /><br />I thought this was well written, easy for me to follow. The technicalities of microbiology were explained at a level that did not cause my eyes to glaze over. I found the portrayal of the scientists interesting, enough of each ones back story to make me appreciate them and cheer for their success.<br /><br />I did not read the chapter or two on the 1976 flu, with its political ramifications. I could not bear the thought of slogging through that mess so I just skipped it. Otherwise, a quick read with many interesting facts and implications for the future.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18208523637193159878">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <a href="/user/show/4170480-elizabeth" class="left imgcol" title="Elizabeth"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1416718508p2/4170480.jpg" alt="Elizabeth" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1445575629?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 18, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4170480-elizabeth" class="user" title="Elizabeth">Elizabeth</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/763331.Flu" title="Paperback 9780743203982" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1445575629">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1725913222688961198">Unfortunately I found the writing horribly awkward and clunky. And worst of all for me, extremely repetitive and long-winded. I'm fairly certain the book could have been at least a third shorter if the redundancies, unnecessary re-explanations, barely related tangents, and overly wordy sentences had been pruned. It brings to mind the way I was taught to write as a history major in college and so many dry history books I had to read: more words are always better, and it's good to restate the same</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1725913222688961198">Unfortunately I found the writing horribly awkward and clunky. And worst of all for me, extremely repetitive and long-winded. I'm fairly certain the book could have been at least a third shorter if the redundancies, unnecessary re-explanations, barely related tangents, and overly wordy sentences had been pruned. It brings to mind the way I was taught to write as a history major in college and so many dry history books I had to read: more words are always better, and it's good to restate the same things several times with slightly different wording. The two stars are for the wonderful book this could have been if the fascinating science and history had not been buried beneath all that terrible prose.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1725913222688961198">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/59521283" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/286835-tamara" class="left imgcol" title="Tamara"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1241014236p2/286835.jpg" alt="Tamara" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/59521283?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 13, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/286835-tamara" class="user" title="Tamara">Tamara</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/763331.Flu" title="Paperback 9780743203982" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer59521283">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10975226832136993392">A fascinating book about the 1918 "spanish" flu pandemic that swept the globe, killing an estimated 20 million to more than 100 million people worldwide. The virus was most deadly to adults aged 20 to 40 - a portion of the population not usually as vulnerable to infectious disease. The death toll was so high that in the United States the average life expectancy dropped by 12 years.<br /><br />The book explores the spread of the virus and the search for it remnants in tissue samples to discover why it was so</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10975226832136993392">A fascinating book about the 1918 "spanish" flu pandemic that swept the globe, killing an estimated 20 million to more than 100 million people worldwide. The virus was most deadly to adults aged 20 to 40 - a portion of the population not usually as vulnerable to infectious disease. The death toll was so high that in the United States the average life expectancy dropped by 12 years.<br /><br />The book explores the spread of the virus and the search for it remnants in tissue samples to discover why it was so lethal and if a vaccine can be created against future outbreaks of this particular strain.<br /><br />Although there is a little more scientific discussion than is probably necessary for the average lay-reader, don't be scared off! This was an interesting read, stunning in its telling and well worth the time! </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10975226832136993392">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1304751006">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1304751006" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/40599887-chuck-tulloh" class="left imgcol" title="Chuck Tulloh"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" alt="Chuck Tulloh" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1304751006?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 10, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/40599887-chuck-tulloh" class="user" title="Chuck Tulloh">Chuck Tulloh</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/763331.Flu" title="Paperback 9780743203982" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1304751006">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9233119266511312827">Good book, provides a well done procedural a la the "police" procedural, discussing the unraveling of the 1918 flu's timeline. Would like a bit more science, and the tantalizing discussion of the possibility that this bug preexisted its catastrophic emergence left me hanging. This is a huge subject so I shouldn't criticize. The personal side was absent and I'm told those truly interested should read Pale Horse Pale Rider as well as Crosby's book America's Forgotten Pandemic to fill in the gaps.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9233119266511312827">Good book, provides a well done procedural a la the "police" procedural, discussing the unraveling of the 1918 flu's timeline. Would like a bit more science, and the tantalizing discussion of the possibility that this bug preexisted its catastrophic emergence left me hanging. This is a huge subject so I shouldn't criticize. The personal side was absent and I'm told those truly interested should read Pale Horse Pale Rider as well as Crosby's book America's Forgotten Pandemic to fill in the gaps. <br />As a side note the discussion about the limitations of "governmental" involvement is scary. The political and legal impediments/ ramifications of ramping up a well intentioned swine flu vaccine program in 1976 and the fact that Dr Johan Hultin could only prospect alone and self financed in Alaska, for the frozen bug in long deceased victims, is just demoralizing. In the wake of the CDC's recent slips including with Anthrax and Ebola, we have three data points that suggest our societal protections as administered by the government aren't up to the task.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9233119266511312827">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1200887799">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1200887799" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1216935-nolan" class="left imgcol" title="Nolan"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" alt="Nolan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1200887799?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 14, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1216935-nolan" class="user" title="Nolan">Nolan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/763331.Flu" title="Paperback 9780743203982" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1216935-nolan?shelf=nls-audio" class="actionLinkLite">nls-audio</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1200887799">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16809154078677738000">When the 24-hour news channels developed a morbid fascination with the Ebola virus last summer, I must admit, I did, too. Being an ardent believer in the premise that we best deal with an uncertain future if we better understand our past, I got to digging around and found this fascinating book.<br /><br />Essentially, Kolata presents you with a highly readable account of the 1918 flu pandemic that killed millions of people worldwide, including millions of American young adults.<br /><br />I read with horror about death</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16809154078677738000">When the 24-hour news channels developed a morbid fascination with the Ebola virus last summer, I must admit, I did, too. Being an ardent believer in the premise that we best deal with an uncertain future if we better understand our past, I got to digging around and found this fascinating book.<br /><br />Essentially, Kolata presents you with a highly readable account of the 1918 flu pandemic that killed millions of people worldwide, including millions of American young adults.<br /><br />I read with horror about death tolls growing at such a rapid rate that people stopped attending funerals of their dead in large cities, piling bodies up outside in some cases for sanitation wagons to take away—and folks, this was in an American city, not some third-world place whose name we can barely pronounce.<br /><br />If a plague of the same virility struck today, an estimated 1.5 million Americans would die. The flue killed more U.S. soldiers than did all of the battles of World War I. in which they participated.<br /><br />If you enjoy a good mystery, you’ll like this book, since it focuses on the detective work necessary to find this disease. Indeed, this particular strain was located by scientists at a national military tissue storage facility and in a remote Alaskan cemetery inside the remains of a plus-sized Eskimo woman.<br /><br />Kolata speculates on what could happen should an airborne killer disease strike again, and her perspective is sobering indeed.<br /><br />If I have any criticism of this book, it is that it didn’t focus hard enough on the societal changes and impact of the 1918 flu. We had a killer on our hands, and we don’t really know where it started or why it mysteriously ended. Granted, we think that some kind of bird flu virus blended with a disease borne by pigs, and that deadly blend was carried to a human who spread what would become a killer that makes the bubonic plague rather inconsequential by comparison.<br /><br />There is some science in here—you can’t have a book like this and not touch on the science of germ creation, cell penetration, etc., and of course, the stories of the competing scientists who wanted to find the virus are both interesting and unsettling.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16809154078677738000">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1116243546">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1116243546" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/37353168-madeleine-bousquet" class="left imgcol" title="Madeleine Bousquet"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Madeleine Bousquet" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1116243546?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 26, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/37353168-madeleine-bousquet" class="user" title="Madeleine Bousquet">Madeleine Bousquet</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/763331.Flu" title="Paperback 9780743203982" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1116243546">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5984111372981285399">Flu: The Story of the Great Influenza Pandemic of 1918 and the Search for the Virus that Caused It by Gina Kolata presents a comprehensive history of the 1918 epidemic, starting from the days of the epidemic and going up to modern attempts to understand the virus’ genome. The author constructs the book as detective story and manages to stick to the theme pretty well. I enjoyed that the book did not just focus on the epidemic itself, but also covered future events to understand why the epidemic h</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5984111372981285399">Flu: The Story of the Great Influenza Pandemic of 1918 and the Search for the Virus that Caused It by Gina Kolata presents a comprehensive history of the 1918 epidemic, starting from the days of the epidemic and going up to modern attempts to understand the virus’ genome. The author constructs the book as detective story and manages to stick to the theme pretty well. I enjoyed that the book did not just focus on the epidemic itself, but also covered future events to understand why the epidemic happened and why it was so infectious. I enjoyed learning about lot about the future scientific experiments on the virus, such as Johan V. Hultin’s attempt to replicate the virus from frozen tissue in 1951. I liked learning about the 1976 swine flu scare, which I knew almost nothing about.<br /><br /><br />I enjoyed that the book was a little more scientifically oriented than other books of the type I have read-- that may be a turnoff to readers with a mores casual/layman interest than myself. When my friend was reading it, she asked me to explain scientific concepts a few times. The book was also a little repetitive and I didn’t love the author’s phrasing either. On the whole, I enjoyed this book. I found the first half of the book more enjoyable that the second, but still recommend this book to anyone interested in infectious disease or immunuology.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5984111372981285399">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1117787817">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5337904222385967115">I thought this was a fascinating look at a subject that I didn't know enough about. The chapters each had a theme and the writing was clear with technical and scientific situations explained well.<br /><br />I had two issues with the book: (1) It could have used tighter editing as some chapters had a lot of repetition, specifically with phrases. (2) One of the chapters described a scientist who organized an expedition as having "doe eyes" and "undeniably attractive to some of the men on her team". The descr</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5337904222385967115">I thought this was a fascinating look at a subject that I didn't know enough about. The chapters each had a theme and the writing was clear with technical and scientific situations explained well.<br /><br />I had two issues with the book: (1) It could have used tighter editing as some chapters had a lot of repetition, specifically with phrases. (2) One of the chapters described a scientist who organized an expedition as having "doe eyes" and "undeniably attractive to some of the men on her team". The description really took me out of the narrative and I felt it didn't not fit the serious theme of the book. It made it seem as though the author had a bias.<br /><br />Overall, this is a great overview of a critical event and search for answers, with clear explanations that neither talked down nor contained an overload of information. Two quibbles aside, I recommend Flu if you wish to learn more about the pandemic of 1918.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1324798480">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16883117295806098198">This is a frightening story. The influenza epidemic of 1918 killed 100 million people worldwide. Could it happen again? Some dedicated researchers spent years trying to resurrect and study the deadly virus in order to answer that question. Their project took them to an Alaskan village where most of the 80 residents succumbed to the virus and to Arctic reaches of Norway to exhume the frozen corpses of seven unlucky sailors; all this to find a piece of frozen lung tissue from which the virus could</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16883117295806098198">This is a frightening story. The influenza epidemic of 1918 killed 100 million people worldwide. Could it happen again? Some dedicated researchers spent years trying to resurrect and study the deadly virus in order to answer that question. Their project took them to an Alaskan village where most of the 80 residents succumbed to the virus and to Arctic reaches of Norway to exhume the frozen corpses of seven unlucky sailors; all this to find a piece of frozen lung tissue from which the virus could be extracted and sequenced through DNA analysis. I won't tell you what they found but let's just say that viruses are tricky little devils. They mutate in order to survive in different environments and sometimes jump to different species. I am somewhat enlightened by this book, but very uneasy with my newly acquired understanding of viruses.</span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1126726876">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8990077258639612251">"Flu" is a surprisingly accessible medical history. Kolata brings the past horror of the 1918 flu to life, then describes the hunt for the virus and what made it so deadly. <br /><br />It hasn't been quite 100 years since the 1918 flu. There have been scares since then, but we've become rather complacent because there aren't very many people left who remember the 1918 epidemic. No one was untouched. I don't think the world is much better equipped to respond to a communicable disease of a similar scale, desp</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8990077258639612251">"Flu" is a surprisingly accessible medical history. Kolata brings the past horror of the 1918 flu to life, then describes the hunt for the virus and what made it so deadly. <br /><br />It hasn't been quite 100 years since the 1918 flu. There have been scares since then, but we've become rather complacent because there aren't very many people left who remember the 1918 epidemic. No one was untouched. I don't think the world is much better equipped to respond to a communicable disease of a similar scale, despite vaccines and developments in supportive care. Ebola has shown us that.<br /><br />All in all, I enjoyed "Flu." Kolata has a clear writing style and she doesn't dwell too much in the minutiae of medical-ese.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_303567334">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/303567334" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1796606-angie" class="left imgcol" title="Angie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1328989261p2/1796606.jpg" alt="Angie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/303567334?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 07, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1796606-angie" class="user" title="Angie">Angie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/763331.Flu" title="Paperback 9780743203982" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer303567334">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14320891409491964136">I think this book did a pretty good job of what is almost 100 years of history related to the flu, and to the advancements scientists have made in figuring out the flu and the 1918 flu in particular... which isn't to say they've actually Figured It Out, it's to say that scientists have managed to find out the code for that virus, but not how it spread, where it came from, where it went. So the mystery is still out there, and seeing as this book was published in 1999 I'm pretty curious as to the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14320891409491964136">I think this book did a pretty good job of what is almost 100 years of history related to the flu, and to the advancements scientists have made in figuring out the flu and the 1918 flu in particular... which isn't to say they've actually Figured It Out, it's to say that scientists have managed to find out the code for that virus, but not how it spread, where it came from, where it went. So the mystery is still out there, and seeing as this book was published in 1999 I'm pretty curious as to the advances made since then.<br /><br />I've been asked to summarize this book and what it contains, but at the same time I'm not sure I fully can without a second read-through. The 1918 flu killed more people than World War I, but was relatively unstudied for years. One scientist began in the early 1950s, digging up frozen flu victims in Alaska, but had to wait and lurk for years before he found a paper indicating that science had advanced enough to use the samples he could get- basically almost 50 years of biding his time before technology had caught up to the samples available.<br /><br />Flu is cyclic, and mutates, and it appears that much like chicken pox the older you are the harder it hits- although if you're old enough you've probably got antibodies from the last flu cycle so are less affected, but the appearance is of a nasty flu attacking the healthiest and Oh god why?!? when it's simply a function of the flu cycle: Older people went through the flu of 15 or 20 or even 50 years ago so are much less affected. But since the virus mutates, even those people could get a similar flu a second time around and be hit hard. I think that's the best summary I can give of the concept of flu; for the particulars I do highly recommend this book and probably a read-through of the source materials, etc. </span>

          </span>
        </div>
